Basic Concepts"}]},{"type":"h3","children":[{"type":"text","text":"1.1 Transactions and Price"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The economy is the sum of all transactions. Each transaction consists of a buyer using money or credit to exchange for goods, services, or financial assets from a seller."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Two basic equations:"}]},{"type":"code_block","value":"总支出（Total Spending）= 货币支出 + 信用支出","children":[{"type":"code_line","children":[{"text":"总支出（Total Spending）= 货币支出 + 信用支出"}]}]},{"type":"code_block","value":"价格（Price）= 总支出 ÷ 售出的商品总量","children":[{"type":"code_line","children":[{"text":"价格（Price）= 总支出 ÷ 售出的商品总量"}]}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"💡 "},{"type":"text","text":"One person's spending is another person's income.","bold":true}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"This equation is the underlying logic for understanding cycle transmission and crisis spread: any entity reducing spending will directly reduce others' income, and reduced income will trigger further spending cuts, forming a chain reaction."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"To understand the price equation within the debt cycle: the total quantity of goods (denominator) changes slowly, so sharp price fluctuations almost entirely come from changes in total spending (numerator), and changes in spending almost entirely come from the expansion and contraction of credit. This is why debt cycles directly manifest as economic booms and busts."}]},{"type":"h3","children":[{"type":"text","text":"1.2 Money and Credit"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Most of what circulates in the economy is not money, but credit."}]},{"type":"table","children":[{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Money"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Credit"}]}]}]},{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Nature"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Hard currency, immediate settlement"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Promise to repay, future settlement"}]}]}]},{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Creation Method"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Only central banks can print"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Any two parties can create it out of thin air"}]}]}]},{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Disappearance Method"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Requires active withdrawal"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Can disappear instantly"}]}]}]}],"props":{"align":[]}},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Once credit is created, it immediately generates "},{"type":"text","text":"debt","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":": it is an "},{"type":"text","text":"asset","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" for the lender, and a "},{"type":"text","text":"liability","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" for the borrower. When the debt, including principal and interest, is repaid, both this asset and liability disappear, and the transaction ends."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"⚠️ Credit can be born instantly and disappear instantly. This is the mechanistic reason why crises come so quickly — it's not that money disappears, but that credit collapses."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The quality of credit depends on its use: credit used for advanced consumption (e.g., buying a TV) cannot generate income to repay debt, which is bad credit; credit used for productive investment (e.g., buying a tractor) can generate income, repay debt, and improve living standards, which is good credit."}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Three Ways Credit Disappears"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Understanding the mechanism of credit disappearance is crucial for assessing the severity of a crisis."}]},{"type":"ol","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Normal Repayment: The borrower repays the principal and interest on time, the asset and liability naturally disappear, with no negative impact on the economy."}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Default: The borrower cannot repay, and the lender's \"asset\" shrinks or becomes zero."}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Voluntary Contraction due to Confidence Collapse: This is the most impactful and easily overlooked type during a crisis. No one needs to actually default — as long as lenders start worrying that borrowers might not be able to repay, they will stop new lending; borrowers, due to uncertain prospects, will also voluntarily stop taking on new debt. The rate of new credit creation falls to zero or even negative, while old credit is gradually repaid, and the total amount of credit rapidly shrinks."}]}]}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"During a crisis, three directions worsen simultaneously:"}]},{"type":"ul","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Newly created credit → approaches zero"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Normally repaid credit → continuously consumed"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Defaulted credit disappearing → begins to increase"}]}]}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Total credit = new creation - normal repayment - default disappearance. All three worsen simultaneously, leading to extremely rapid contraction."}]},{"type":"h3","children":[{"type":"text","text":"1.3 Nominal vs. Real Values"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"When analyzing economic data, two perspectives must be distinguished:"}]},{"type":"ul","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Nominal Value = book value, not considering inflation"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Real Value ≈ Nominal Value - Inflation Rate, reflecting true purchasing power"}]}]}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"When it comes to income, spending, and debt burden, "},{"type":"text","text":"nominal values","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" are used to describe mechanisms and transmission (debt contracts are written in nominal figures; if you borrow 1 million, you must repay 1 million, regardless of inflation); "},{"type":"text","text":"real values","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" are used to assess actual living standards and debt pressure."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Example 1: Nominal Wage vs. Real Wage","bold":true}]},{"type":"table","children":[{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Situation"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Nominal Wage Increase"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Inflation Rate"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Real Wage Change"}]}]}]},{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Appears to have a raise"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"+3%"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"+5%"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"-2% (actually poorer)"}]}]}]},{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Appears no raise"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"0%"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"-2% (deflation)"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"+2% (actually richer)"}]}]}]},{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"True Growth"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"+5%"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"+2%"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"+3%"}]}]}]}],"props":{"align":[]}},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Example 2: Asymmetric Effect of Inflation on Debt","bold":true}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Inflation has an asymmetric effect on debtors and creditors:"}]},{"type":"ul","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Inflation is "},{"type":"text","text":"beneficial to debtors","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":": repaying debt with \"cheaper money,\" actual debt burden decreases"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Deflation is a "},{"type":"text","text":"disaster for debtors","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":": nominal income decreases, but nominal debt remains unchanged, so the actual debt burden increases instead"}]}]}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"This is the fundamental reason why central banks choose to create moderate inflation during deleveraging — quietly diluting the real value of society's debt."}]},{"type":"h3","children":[{"type":"text","text":"1.4 Five Economic Entities"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"All economic activities are completed by five types of entities through division of labor. Understanding the function of each entity is necessary to comprehend \"who is doing what\" in the cycle."}]},{"type":"table","children":[{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Entity"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Core Function"}]}]}]},{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Individuals/Households"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Consumption, contraction"}]}]}]},{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Businesses"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Investment, Banks"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Decide whether to lend, are the direct channel for credit creation, and are also damaged in crises"}]}]}]},{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Central Government"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Tax, spend, issue debt, needs to expand deficits to inject demand during necessary"}]}]}]}],"props":{"align":[]}},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"⚠️ Only the central bank has the right to \"print money.\" Commercial banks can only decide whether to lend, they cannot create base money."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The credit chain among these five entities is: Central Bank sets interest rates (regulating the cost of credit) → Commercial Banks decide to lend (creating credit) → Individuals and Businesses borrow for consumption and investment (using credit) → Central Government directly intervenes when the credit system fails (replacing credit)."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Who is the Main Actor in Different wealth. Diligence, innovation, and knowledge accumulation continuously improve living standards, which cannot be replaced by borrowing."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"However, productivity growth is slow and stable, with little short-term fluctuation, thus it is not the main driver of "},{"type":"text","text":"short-term economic fluctuations","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":". Short-term fluctuations are primarily credit-driven."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Investment implication: Debt-driven growth has a ceiling; only a genuine increase in productivity can continuously raise living standards."}]},{"type":"h3","children":[{"type":"text","text":"2.3 The Essence of Lending"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Lending = bringing future purchasing power forward to be used now."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Borrowing means consuming more than income now, and in the future, one must consume less than income to repay debt. This naturally forms a cycle. Borrowing at any time initiates a mechanical, predictable chain of future events — which is the fundamental reason for the existence of short-term and long-term debt cycles."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"How much one can borrow depends on "},{"type":"text","text":"creditworthiness","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":", which consists of two conditions:"}]},{"type":"ul","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Ability to Repay: The level of income relative to debt"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Collateral: Assets that can be used to repay debt in case of non-repayment"}]}]}]},{"type":"h2","children":[{"type":"text","text":"3. Short-Term Debt Cycle"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The short-term debt cycle (5–8 years) is regulated by the central bank through interest rates. As long as there is room for interest rate adjustment, this cycle can be managed."}]},{"type":"ol","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Expansion","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" Phase"}]},{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Easy access to credit → Increased spending (nominal terms) → Increased income → Rising prices → Inflation"}]},{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The expansion phase has a "},{"type":"text","text":"self-reinforcing cycle","italic":true},{"type":"text","text":": increased credit → increased spending → increased income for others → improved creditworthiness → ability to borrow more → further increased spending. This positive feedback (Self-Reinforcing Pattern) continuously drives economic expansion."}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Central Bank Tightening","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" Phase"}]},{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Central bank does not want inflation to be too high → Raises interest rates → Cost of new borrowing increases; monthly payments for floating-rate debt increase → Lending willingness decreases, spending of some debtors is compressed → Income decreases"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Recession","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" Phase"}]},{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Sustained decrease in spending → Reduced upward pressure on prices (deflation occurs in severe cases) → Decreased economic activity"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Central Bank Easing","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" Phase"}]},{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Recession is too severe → Central bank lowers interest rates → Lending willingness recovers (premise: debt burden has not reached structural limits) → Spending recovers → New round of expansion"}]}]}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"📌 At the end of each short-term cycle, the total debt is higher than in the previous cycle — because people are inherently inclined to borrow more and repay less. This is the structural source of long-term debt cycle accumulation."}]},{"type":"h3","children":[{"type":"text","text":"3.1 Inflation and Deflation"}]},{"type":"ul","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Inflation: Decreased purchasing power of money, sustained increase in overall price levels."}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Deflation: Increased purchasing power of money, sustained decrease in overall price levels."}]}]}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Note the word \"sustained\" — individual commodity price fluctuations are not inflation/deflation; it refers to a systemic change in the overall price level."}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Causes of Inflation","bold":true}]},{"type":"code_block","value":"价格 = (信用 + 货币) ÷ 售出的商品总量","children":[{"type":"code_line","children":[{"text":"价格 = (信用 + 货币) ÷ 售出的商品总量"}]}]},{"type":"ul","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Demand-Pull: Credit expansion → Total spending exceeds production capacity → Numerator (credit) increases → Prices rise. This is the main character in Dalio's framework."}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Cost-Push: Rising production costs → Companies cannot maintain original output at the same price, raise prices to pass on costs → Denominator shrinks → Prices rise. This type of inflation is not accompanied by increased demand, but rather compresses consumption, leading to a decrease in real purchasing power."}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Monetary Over-issuance: Money supply growth far exceeds actual output growth → Numerator (money) increases → Prices rise. A typical mechanism for hyperinflation, Germany in the 1920s is a textbook example."}]}]}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Causes of Deflation"}]},{"type":"code_block","value":"价格 = (信用 + 货币) ÷ 售出的商品总量","children":[{"type":"code_line","children":[{"text":"价格 = (信用 + 货币) ÷ 售出的商品总量"}]}]},{"type":"ul","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Credit Collapse (most dangerous): During deleveraging, a large amount of credit instantly disappears → Total spending plummets → Prices plummet, extremely fast, self-reinforcing."}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Productivity Improvement (benign): Technological progress improves production efficiency → Supply increases → Prices naturally fall. This type of deflation is not a bad thing. "},{"type":"text","text":"Example:","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" Over the past few decades, electronic products have continuously decreased in price, backed by advancements in semiconductor technology, improving living standards."}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Long-term Demand Contraction: Aging population, long-term low consumption willingness → Sustained insufficient demand → Decreased corporate income, low investment willingness → Shrinking lending demand, reduced credit creation → Contraction of total money/credit in circulation → Further decrease in total spending → Downward prices → Deflationary expectations form → Consumption further postponed. Japan is a typical example."}]}]}]},{"type":"table","children":[{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Good Deflation"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Bad Deflation"}]}]}]},{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Cause"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Increased productivity, increased supply"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Credit collapse or long-term demand contraction"}]}]}]},{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Result"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Improved living standards"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Economic downward spiral"}]}]}]},{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Needs intervention?"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"No"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Yes"}]}]}]}],"props":{"align":[]}},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Common Indicators"}]},{"type":"table","children":[{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Indicator"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Measures"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Description"}]}]}]},{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Consumer Price Index; CPI"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Change in prices of a basket of goods purchased by ordinary households"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Most commonly used"}]}]}]},{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Producer Price Index; PPI"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Change in prices of raw materials and intermediate goods in the production process"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Leading indicator for CPI"}]}]}]},{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Personal Consumption Expenditures; PCE"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Broader coverage of consumer prices"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Fed's preferred"}]}]}]}],"props":{"align":[]}},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"PPI leads CPI: Rising production costs often take a few months to transmit to consumer prices. Looking at PPI can help predict CPI trends in advance."}]},{"type":"h3","children":[{"type":"text","text":"3.2 The Central Bank's Toolkit"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Tool One: Interest Rate Regulation","bold":true}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Interest rate is the price of borrowing money. Adjusting interest rates is equivalent to adjusting the cost of borrowing, which in turn affects borrowing willingness, and ultimately affects total spending."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Transmission chain: Interest rate cut → Borrowing becomes cheaper → Borrowing willingness increases → Spending increases → Economic expansion"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Tool Two: Quantitative Easing (QE)","bold":true}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"When interest rates have fallen to extremely low levels and traditional tools become ineffective, the central bank needs to switch to another set of tools."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The name QE comes from the Japanese "},{"type":"text","text":"量的緩和 (ryōteki kanwa)","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":", first pioneered by the Bank of Japan in 2001. Ordinary interest rate cuts adjust the "},{"type":"text","text":"price","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" of borrowing money (interest rates), while QE adjusts the "},{"type":"text","text":"quantity","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" of money supply (directly expanding the central bank's balance sheet) — switching from \"price regulation\" to \"quantity regulation,\" which is the meaning of \"quantitative.\""}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"How QE works: The central bank directly buys assets (mainly government bonds, mortgage-backed securities) in the open market → Injects money into the banking system → Banks have more funds to lend → Simultaneously pushes down long-term interest rates."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Common Limitations of Both Tools","bold":true}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Both sets of tools rely on banks being willing to lend, and businesses and individuals being willing to borrow. If the economy is in a deleveraging phase, and everyone wants to repay debt rather than borrow, no matter how low interest rates are or how abundant money is in banks, the transmission chain will break at the \"willingness to borrow\" link."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Another important boundary: the central bank can only purchase "},{"type":"text","text":"financial assets","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":", it cannot directly distribute money to ordinary people. Therefore, QE money first enters financial markets, pushing up stock, bond, and real estate prices. Groups holding these assets (usually the wealthy) benefit more, while the consumer side feels limited impact. This is the mechanistic reason why QE after 2008 pushed up asset prices, but wealth inequality simultaneously widened."}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Why Central Banks Aim for an Inflation Target of Around 2%"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Harms of excessively high inflation:"}]},{"type":"ol","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Erodes purchasing power, especially harming fixed-income groups"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Distorted price signals, decreased efficiency of resource allocation"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Systematically penalizes savings, encourages excessive borrowing"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Inflation has self-reinforcing characteristics; once out of control, it enters hyperinflation"}]}]}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Harms of excessively low inflation (deflation):"}]},{"type":"ul","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Triggers a \"postponed consumption\" mentality — consumption delayed → corporate income decreases → layoffs → fewer people consume → prices continue to fall. This is a deflationary spiral, in which Japan was deeply mired in the 1990s."}]}]}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"2% is a buffer between two extremes: low enough not to distort the economy; high enough to preserve room for monetary policy adjustment (interest rates won't easily be forced to zero), and simultaneously provide debtors with breathing room."}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The Dilemma of Imported Inflation"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Imported Inflation refers to inflation \"imported\" from abroad — domestic monetary policy and debt cycles are normal, but rising import prices lead to a passive increase in the overall domestic price level."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Trigger mechanism: Rising commodity prices (e.g., oil, food) or currency depreciation → Increased import costs → Companies raise prices to pass on costs → Overall price level passively increases, unrelated to domestic demand."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"This puts central banks in a dilemma: the tool to combat inflation is raising interest rates (suppressing domestic credit expansion), but the root cause of imported inflation is not domestic credit. Raising interest rates would only suppress borrowing and consumption, push up unemployment, while import prices would still rise, and inflation would not disappear. This state is called "},{"type":"text","text":"Stagflation","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" — economic stagnation + inflation occurring simultaneously, which is the most difficult scenario for central bank tools to handle. The 1970s US oil crisis is a textbook example."}]},{"type":"h2","children":[{"type":"text","text":"4. Long-Term Debt Cycle"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The long-term debt cycle is the longest (75–100 years) and most destructive force. It is not a simple amplified version of the short-term debt cycle, but rather the structural result of dozens of short-term cycles superimposed and accumulated. Understanding it requires grasping four consecutive stages: leverage accumulation → peak → deleveraging → reflexive recovery."}]},{"type":"h3","children":[{"type":"text","text":"4.1 Leverage Accumulation Phase"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Structural Starting Point: Each Short-Term Cycle Leaves More Debt","bold":true}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"At the end of the short-term debt cycle, the central bank stimulates a new round of expansion by cutting interest rates. However, at the start of each expansion, the total debt is slightly higher than in the previous round — because people are inherently inclined to borrow more and repay less, and the troughs of short-term cycles never fully clear the debt accumulated from the previous round. After dozens of short-term cycles, this \"leaving a little each time\" effect accumulates into a massive debt stock."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Core Mechanism of Self-Reinforcement: Positive Feedback between Collateral and Credit","bold":true}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Increased borrowing → Increased demand for asset purchases (stocks, real estate) → Rising asset prices → Increased collateral value → Improved creditworthiness → Ability to borrow more → Further rising asset prices → ……"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"This cycle can sustain itself for a long time. As long as asset prices are rising, the debt burden appears manageable — net worth (assets minus liabilities) increases, and people feel increasingly wealthy. This is why bubbles can last for decades."}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Core Indicator for Measuring Debt Pressure"}]},{"type":"code_block","value":"债务负担（Debt Burden）= 债务还款额 ÷ 收入","children":[{"type":"code_line","children":[{"text":"债务负担（Debt Burden）= 债务还款额 ÷ 收入"}]}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Note that \"repayment amount\" is used here, not \"total debt\" — the real pressure comes not from the stock of debt, but from the proportion of income that must be repaid each period. As long as income growth can cover repayment growth, this cycle can continue."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"However, debt accumulation has an inherent asymmetry: borrowing can be done in a day, but repayment takes years; asset prices rise quickly during credit expansion, but fall quickly once reversed. This dictates that the long-term debt cycle must have a peak."}]},{"type":"h3","children":[{"type":"text","text":"4.2 Long-Term Debt Peak"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Trigger Condition: Repayment Speed Exceeds Income Growth Rate","bold":true}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The Long-Term Debt Peak is not caused by a single sudden event, but rather the natural eruption of internal pressure after debt accumulates to a certain critical point:"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Debt repayment growth rate > Income growth rate → Net decrease in money available for consumption and investment → Total spending (nominal terms) decreases → Others' income decreases → Creditworthiness declines → Lending capacity decreases → Asset prices begin to fall → Collateral value shrinks → Creditworthiness further deteriorates → Cycle reverses"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Early Signals of the Peak Phase","bold":true}]},{"type":"ul","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Asset prices stop rising, trading volume shrinks"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Credit standards quietly tighten, marginal borrowers begin to be rejected for loans"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Debt repayment as a percentage of income reaches historical highs"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Nominal income growth rate begins to fall below debt interest rates"}]}]}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The characteristic of the peak is: even without external shocks, internal pressure is already sufficient to trigger a reversal. External shocks (such as central bank interest rate hikes, market panic) often merely ignite the fuse, rather than creating the problem."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Historical references: US 1929, Japan 1989, US 2008."}]},{"type":"h3","children":[{"type":"text","text":"4.3 Deleveraging"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Essential Difference from Recession","bold":true}]},{"type":"table","children":[{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Recession"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Deleveraging"}]}]}]},{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Trigger Cause"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Short-term credit tightening"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Debt burden accumulated to structural limits"}]}]}]},{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Interest Rate Level"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Still room for reduction"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Extremely low or even zero, stimulus effect ineffective"}]}]}]},{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Problem Essence"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Borrowing costs too high"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Total debt itself too heavy, borrowers do not want to borrow more"}]}]}]},{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Central Bank Tools"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Interest rate cuts effective"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Interest rate cuts ineffective"}]}]}]},{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Metaphor"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Fever, fever reducer works"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Organs starting to fail, fever reducer not enough"}]}]}]}],"props":{"align":[]}},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Interest rate cuts are effective during a recession because the problem lies in borrowing costs. During deleveraging, interest rate cuts are ineffective because the problem is not the cost, but that debtors no longer want to take on new debt — their primary task is to repay old debts and repair their balance sheets. No matter how low interest rates are, they cannot change the reality of \"debt must be repaid.\""}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Typical Symptoms of Deleveraging","bold":true}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"During the deleveraging phase, multiple negative feedback loops start simultaneously and reinforce each other:"}]},{"type":"ul","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Spending decreases → Income decreases → Creditworthiness declines → Lending decreases → Spending further decreases"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Meanwhile: Asset prices plummet → Collateral value shrinks → Bank balance sheets deteriorate → Banks tighten lending → More borrowers unable to refinance → Forced to sell assets → Prices further fall"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Societal level: Rising unemployment → Intensified social conflicts → Rising extreme political risks"}]}]}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Four Responses","bold":true}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"There are no shortcuts to deleveraging. The essence of the problem is that the total debt exceeds the economy's capacity to digest it, and can only be reduced through the following four methods:"}]},{"type":"table","children":[{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Economic Effect"}]}]}]},{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Spending Cuts (Austerity)"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Deflationary"}]}]}]},{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Debt Restructuring"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Deflationary"}]}]}]},{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Wealth Redistribution (Taxing the rich)"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Deflationary"}]}]}]},{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Central Bank Printing Money"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Inflationary"}]}]}]}],"props":{"align":[]}},{"type":"ol","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The Paradox of Austerity: Spending cuts trigger the reverse chain of \"one person's spending is another's income\"\nA cuts spending → B's income decreases (nominal) → B is also forced to cut → C's income decreases → ……\nWhen the entire society cuts simultaneously, income falls faster than debt repayment, and the debt burden ("},{"type":"text","text":"Debt ÷ Income","code":true},{"type":"text","text":") actually rises. This stage can lead to an economic depression."}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The Essence of Debt Restructuring: Lenders accept principal write-downs, extended maturities, or lower interest rates in exchange for partial recovery (A little of something is better than all of nothing).\nHowever, debt restructuring is also deflationary: while debt is extinguished, corresponding assets are also extinguished, total purchasing power further shrinks, potentially triggering bank runs."}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The Political Difficulty of Wealth Redistribution: Taxing the wealthy transfers resources to low- and middle-income groups, but under the social pressures of deleveraging, political friction is immense. Mishandling can lead to the rise of extreme politics — 1930s Germany is an example."}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The Limits of Central Bank Money Printing: The central bank can only purchase financial assets and cannot directly distribute money to ordinary people. Therefore, it must cooperate with the central government.\nCentral bank buys government bonds → Government financing costs decrease, allowing for expanded budget deficits → Money injected into the real economy through stimulus plans and unemployment benefits → Nominal household income rises → Debt burden relatively decreases\n⚠️ Excessive money printing → Hyperinflation. Germany in the 1920s is a textbook counter-example."}]}]}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Beautiful Deleveraging"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Deflationary tools and inflationary tools are properly balanced, with the two forces offsetting each other → Debt slowly declines relative to income, real economy experiences moderate positive growth, and inflation does not get out of control. This is \"Beautiful Deleveraging.\""}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Mathematical condition for achievement:"}]},{"type":"code_block","value":"名义收入增速 > 债务的名义利率","children":[{"type":"code_line","children":[{"text":"名义收入增速 > 债务的名义利率"}]}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Example:","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" Debt/income ratio is 100%, interest rate is 2%. If nominal income growth is only 1%, the debt burden continuously rises, and deleveraging fails. If nominal income growth reaches 4% (2% from real economic growth, 2% from moderate inflation created by money printing), the debt burden begins to actually decrease."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Key premise: Money printing does not necessarily cause inflation — if the new money merely fills the void left by disappearing credit, and total spending (nominal terms) remains unchanged, prices will not rise. Total spending is the determinant of prices, regardless of whether the spending comes from money or credit."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Complete Cycle Timeline","bold":true}]},{"type":"ul","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Phase One: Deflationary Spiral (most painful)"}]},{"type":"ul","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Asset prices plummet → Credit disappears → Austerity + debt restructuring occur simultaneously"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Nominal income falls faster than debt repayment → Debt burden worsens"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Unemployment soars, social conflicts intensify"}]}]}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Phase Two: Policy Intervention"}]},{"type":"ul","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Central bank cuts interest rates to extremely low levels (already ineffective) → Initiates QE"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Central government expands fiscal deficit, directly injects demand into the economy"}]}]}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Phase Three: Equilibrium Point"}]},{"type":"ul","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Deflationary forces and inflationary forces reach equilibrium"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Nominal income begins to grow, debt burden begins to actually decrease"}]}]}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Phase Four: Reflation"}]},{"type":"ul","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Income rises → Creditworthiness improves → Lending cautiously resumes"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Moderate economic growth, but growth rate is much lower than before deleveraging"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Asset prices slowly recover, often taking longer than the economy"}]}]}]}]}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Full duration: Over 10 years"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The reflation phase is the sign that the cycle has truly completed its loop — it's not that debt has disappeared, but that the debt burden (debt ÷ income) has returned to a sustainable level, and the economy can re-expand on a new, lower leverage ratio. During this phase, interest rates typically need to remain low to ensure that nominal income growth consistently exceeds debt interest rates."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Two Extremes of Imbalanced Allocation:","bold":true}]},{"type":"ul","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Deflationary forces too strong (austerity-led, insufficient money printing) → Depression: Economy continuously contracts, actual debt burden becomes heavier and heavier, falling into a deflationary spiral."}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Inflationary forces too strong (excessive money printing, lack of restraint) → Hyperinflation: Currency and credit collapse, prices spiral out of control."}]}]}]},{"type":"h2","children":[{"type":"text","text":"5. Limits of the Framework"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The core assumption of Dalio's framework is that economic problems originate internally — from excessive credit expansion and contraction. However, in reality, several types of external variables can undermine this assumption, rendering parts or all of the framework's tools ineffective."}]},{"type":"h3","children":[{"type":"text","text":"5.1 Exogenous Supply-Side Shocks"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Supply-side shocks such as wars, natural disasters, and trade blockades directly compress the denominator of the price equation (effective supply), causing inflation. However, this is unrelated to the credit cycle, and the central bank's interest rate hike tools have limited effectiveness."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Example:","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" In February 2026, US-Israeli military strikes on Iran led to the blockade of the Strait of Hormuz, affecting approximately 20% of global oil supply. This created an imported inflation shock for all energy-importing economies, particularly devastating Japan (which relies on the Middle East for about 90% of its crude oil). The International Energy Agency characterized this as the largest global energy supply disruption in history."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The result is often stagflation — central banks face the dilemma of \"raising interest rates to curb inflation but harming growth, or not raising rates and letting inflation spiral out of control,\" with no clean solution."}]},{"type":"h3","children":[{"type":"text","text":"5.2 Demographic Structure"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Population aging leads to structural demand deficiency: older people have low consumption willingness, and medical and pension expenditures crowd out other consumption; a shrinking working population limits the growth of the economy's actual production capacity; fewer younger generations lead to long-term pessimistic expectations for future demand among businesses, deterring investment."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"This is where monetary and fiscal policies cannot reach. The Bank of Japan has maintained ultra-loose policies for a long time, but inflation simply won't pick up — there is a persistent structural leak on the demand side, offsetting the effects created by monetary easing."}]},{"type":"h3","children":[{"type":"text","text":"5.3 Changes in Corporate Behavior"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"After a bubble bursts, companies' primary goal shifts from "},{"type":"text","text":"profit maximization","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" to "},{"type":"text","text":"debt minimization","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":". Even if interest rates fall to zero, companies will not borrow for investment, because repairing balance sheets damaged by excessive leverage is the first priority."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"This breaks the monetary policy transmission chain: central bank easing → banks have money → but companies don't borrow → the real economy doesn't feel it."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Japanese listed companies have long hoarded large amounts of cash, not investing, not distributing dividends, and not raising salaries. Funds are locked up and do not participate in the economic cycle. This is one of the core mechanisms behind the long-term ineffectiveness of Japan's macro-easing policies."}]},{"type":"h3","children":[{"type":"text","text":"5.4 Lack of Structural Reforms"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Productivity growth is the sole source of long-term wealth. However, productivity growth requires structural reforms: labor market flexibility, a competitive business environment, and an innovation ecosystem. These changes proceed extremely slowly due to political and socio-cultural resistance, and are far from being replaceable by monetary and fiscal policies."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"During Japan's lost three decades, lifetime employment protected jobs but hindered talent mobility; cross-shareholding reduced competitive pressure; and the internet era failed to foster a new generation of tech companies. Structural issues are the deepest constraints."}]},{"type":"h2","children":[{"type":"text","text":"6. Application"}]},{"type":"h3","children":[{"type":"text","text":"6.1 Three Principles of Action"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"At the end of the video, Dalio provides three principles, applicable to individuals and policymakers alike. There is an inherent logical hierarchy among the three:"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Principle Three is Fundamental:","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" Do everything possible to increase productivity — productivity growth is the sole source of true wealth, and in the long run, this is the most important thing."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Principle One is the Prerequisite for Protecting the Fundamental:","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" Do not let debt grow faster than income — an excessive debt burden will crush the space for productivity accumulation, leading the economy into deleveraging rather than growth."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Principle Two is to Prevent False Prosperity:","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" Do not let income grow faster than productivity — income that deviates from productivity for too long means debt or bubbles are driving growth, which will eventually lead to a loss of competitiveness and trigger adjustments."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The logical relationship of the three principles: increasing productivity is the goal, controlling debt is to ensure the goal is not undermined, and preventing false income is to avoid going down the wrong path."}]},{"type":"h3","children":[{"type":"text","text":"6.2 Determining Current Cycle Position"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The core value of this framework for investment is not to predict short-term market trends, but to help determine the current macroeconomic stage, avoiding inverse decisions at the wrong cycle position."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Judgment Process","bold":true}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Step One: Observe interest rate trends, qualitatively determine the cycle stage"}]},{"type":"ul","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Interest rates rising → Central bank tightening, in late expansion or overheating phase"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Interest rates falling → Central bank easing, in recession or early deleveraging phase"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Interest rates have bottomed out, tools ineffective → Possibly in deleveraging phase, proceed to step two for confirmation\nStep Two: Look at real wages, confirm transmission direction"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Nominal wage growth > Inflation rate → Real purchasing power is improving, credit expansion is effective"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Nominal wage growth < Inflation rate → Real purchasing power is declining, even if nominal figures look good, it does not represent real improvement\nStep Three: Look at debt burden, determine if it's a short-term or long-term problem"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Debt/GDP is within historical normal range and trend is stable → Short-term debt cycle problem, interest rate cuts and other tools are effective"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Debt/GDP continuously rising and at historical highs → Long-term debt cycle pressure, need to be wary of deleveraging risk\nStep Four: Compare against framework limits, check if tools will fail"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Are there exogenous supply-side shocks (energy, food)?"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Does demographic structure cause a long-term demand gap?"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Are companies hoarding cash instead of investing?"}]}]}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Key Indicator References","bold":true}]},{"type":"ul","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Interest rate levels and trends (hikes/cuts/bottoming out)"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Inflation data (CPI/PCE, whether it exceeds target)"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Real wage changes (nominal wage growth - inflation rate)"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Debt burden (Debt/GDP, historical position and trend)"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Asset price trends (whether in a self-reinforcing cycle)"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Central bank policy signals and rhetoric"}]}]}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"📌 Limitations of this framework: It can help you understand where you are currently, and why certain policies occur, but it cannot precisely predict short-term market trends. Macroeconomic judgment is context, not a timing tool."}]},{"type":"h2","children":[{"type":"text","text":"7. Case Study"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"This section uses the theoretical framework as a basis to test its explanatory power through specific historical cases. The value of the cases lies not in memorizing events, but in understanding: how the same framework logic produced distinctly different results in different countries and different eras."}]},{"type":"h3","children":[{"type":"text","text":"7.1 Japan 1989: Anatomy of a Bubble"}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The Beginning of the Bubble"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Japan's 1989 bubble originated from a geopolitical decision in 1985."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Plaza Accord (September 1985)","bold":true}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The US external trade deficit continued to widen, and the Reagan administration attributed the cause to an overvalued dollar. In September 1985, the United States, Japan, UK, France, and West Germany signed an agreement at the Plaza Hotel in New York to depress the dollar through coordinated foreign exchange intervention."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"When the agreement was signed, the USD to JPY exchange rate was approximately 238:1. Over the next two years, the yen appreciated sharply, reaching 127 at one point in 1987, an appreciation of nearly double."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The direct impact of the yen's appreciation was a collapse in export competitiveness — Japan's exports to the US fell to about half of their 1986 peak by 1992. This recession, triggered by the exchange rate, is known in Japanese as \"円高不況\" (endaka fukyō, strong yen recession)."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Japan's Response: Aggressive Interest Rate Cuts","bold":true}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"To counter the damage to exports, the Bank of Japan (BOJ) continuously cut interest rates starting in 1986, lowering the benchmark discount rate from 5% to 2.5% in February 1987 — a post-war historical low."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The critical policy error was that this extremely low interest rate was maintained for over two full years (February 1987 to May 1989), far exceeding the time needed to address the strong yen recession. In fact, the Japanese economy had already entered expansion in the second half of 1987, but the BOJ, citing international policy coordination (Louvre Accord) and external pressure, was slow to tighten monetary policy."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Mechanism of the Bubble","bold":true}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"In the mature Japanese economy, extremely low interest rates did not flow into new factories and productive investments, but rather into asset markets. This triggered the positive feedback loop described in 4.1:"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Increased borrowing → Increased demand for asset purchases (stocks, real estate) → Rising asset prices → Increased collateral value → Ability to borrow more → Asset prices further rise → ……"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The Bank of Japan also further accelerated credit expansion through its \"Window Guidance\" (窓口指導) mechanism, directly allocating credit quotas to commercial banks. Between 1988 and 1989, the growth rate of funds in the corporate and household sectors approached an annual year-on-year increase of 14%."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Figures at the Peak of the Bubble","bold":true}]},{"type":"ul","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The Nikkei index rose from approximately 13,000 points in 1985 to nearly 38,915 points by the end of 1989, a nearly threefold increase in five years."}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The theoretical market value of land around the Tokyo Imperial Palace was once estimated to be enough to buy the entire state of California, USA."}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Japanese companies engaged in large-scale global acquisitions: Mitsubishi Estate acquired New York's Rockefeller Center in 1989, and Japanese companies bought large amounts of assets in Hawaii and across the United States."}]}]}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"This is a typical form of the long-term debt cycle accumulation phase in Dalio's framework — the debt burden appears manageable because asset prices rise simultaneously; everyone feels increasingly wealthy. This is why bubbles can last for decades."}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Collapse: Interest Rate Hikes Begin in 1989"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The Bank of Japan finally recognized the asset bubble risk in 1989 and began a series of interest rate hikes, raising the discount rate from 2.5% to 6% by August 1990."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Bank of Japan begins raising interest rates → Borrowing costs rise → Asset prices begin to fall → Collateral value shrinks → Banks begin to tighten lending → Forced asset sales → Prices further fall."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"By 1992, Japan's asset bubble had completely burst. The stock market fell by about 60% within three years of its peak. Land prices began to collapse in 1991, with the average price of commercial, residential, and industrial land in the six major cities falling by 15.5% that year, and continuing to decline for nearly two decades thereafter."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"This was the landmark moment of the long-term debt peak. External shocks (interest rate hikes) merely ignited the fuse; the internal unsustainability of the bubble had already taken shape."}]},{"type":"hr","children":[{"type":"text","text":""}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The Lost Three Decades: Why Not Ten Years"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Dalio's framework predicts that deleveraging takes about 10 years. Japan took thirty years, for multi-layered reasons."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Surface Level: Policy Errors","bold":true}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"① Zombie Banks not cleared for a long time"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"After the bubble burst, the Japanese banking system accumulated a large amount of non-performing loans on its books, but banks were unwilling to admit it — admission would mean insufficient capital and the risk of forced restructuring. The result was that banks continuously injected funds into insolvent \"zombie companies,\" maintaining their superficial existence, a practice known as \"evergreening.\""}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Research found that in the early 2000s, approximately 30% of all listed manufacturing, construction, real estate, retail, wholesale, and service companies in Japan were relying on bank transfusions to stay alive."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"These zombie banks tied up significant credit resources but failed to drive genuine economic recovery — they used their limited lending capacity to sustain companies that had no vitality, rather than supporting new industries with potential."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"② Intermittent fiscal stimulus, limited effectiveness"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Throughout the 1990s, the Japanese government launched multiple rounds of fiscal stimulus packages, totaling hundreds of billions of dollars. However, each time stimulus initially showed results, the government tightened policies due to concerns about fiscal deficits, leading to repeated abortive recoveries."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"③ Poor timing for 1997 consumption tax hike"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"In 1997, the Japanese government, to consolidate public finances, raised the consumption tax from 3% to 5%. The timing was chosen just as the Asian financial crisis erupted and the Japanese economy had shown a slight sign of recovery — this tax hike directly suppressed demand, and Japan fell back into recession that year, with the banking sector subsequently experiencing a systemic crisis."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Mid-Level: Deflationary Expectations Locked In","bold":true}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Under deflationary expectations, the value of cash increases over time. In this situation, Japanese companies began cutting wages, R&D, and other investments, opting to hold cash."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"This created a self-reinforcing cycle: consumers expect \"it will be cheaper tomorrow, so don't buy today\" → corporate income falls, companies dare not raise wages or invest → wages don't rise, consumption further shrinks → prices continue to fall → expectations further solidify."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Once deflationary expectations are embedded in the behavior of economic agents, monetary policy loses its transmission channel. Even if interest rates fall to zero, borrowing willingness will not recover — this is precisely the mechanism described by the limitations of the 3.3 toolkit."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Deep Level: Structural Constraints (Beyond the Reach of Monetary Policy)","bold":true}]},{"type":"table","children":[{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Structural Problem"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Specific Manifestation"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Can Monetary/Fiscal Policy Solve It?"}]}]}]},{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Aging Population"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Decreased workforce, low consumption willingness, persistent demand-side leakage"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"❌ Can only alleviate"}]}]}]},{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Change in Corporate Behavior"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Goal shifts from profit maximization to debt minimization, monetary transmission breaks here"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"❌ Requires corporate governance reform"}]}]}]},{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Lifetime Employment"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Protected employment but hindered talent mobility, inefficient companies difficult to be eliminated by market"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"❌ Requires labor market reform"}]}]}]},{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Cross-Shareholding"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Companies protect each other, insufficient competitive pressure, innovation ecosystem stagnates"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"❌ Requires institutional change"}]}]}]}],"props":{"align":[]}},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"In the long run, Japan's per capita GDP still maintained growth, and the unemployment rate remained at a low level — this indicates that the \"lost three decades\" is a relative concept: Japan's performance severely lagged compared to its potential growth and compared to other developed countries during the same period. But it was not a complete economic collapse."}]},{"type":"h3","children":[{"type":"text","text":"7.2 US 2008: How Financial Innovation Severed Risk Constraints"}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Mechanism of the Bubble: Systemic Failure of Risk Transfer"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The 2008 crisis in the US had a fundamental structural difference from Japan in 1989: Japan experienced a traditional bank credit bubble, while the US saw an accumulation of systemic risk created by financial innovation."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Starting Point: Low Interest Rates After the 2000 Dot-Com Bubble Burst","bold":true}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The dot-com bubble burst in 2000, coupled with the impact of the 9/11 attacks in 2001, led the Federal Reserve to continuously lower the federal funds rate from 6.5% to 1% by 2003. This extremely low interest rate environment persisted for a relatively long time, causing large amounts of capital to seek higher returns and flow into the real estate market."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Key Accelerator: Securitization Severed Risk Constraints","bold":true}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Traditional bank lending model: Bank lends → Borrower repays → Bank bears risk, thus has incentive to prudently screen borrowers."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"New securitization model:\nBank lends → Packages loans into Mortgage-Backed Securities (MBS) → Sells to investors → Risk transferred out → Bank can continue to lend more, credit standards continuously lowered"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The securitization rate of subprime mortgages rose from 54% in 2001 to 75% in 2006. Concurrently, the proportion of subprime mortgages in total loan originations increased from less than 10% in 2001–2003 to 18–20% between 2004–2006."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"By mid-2008, over 60% of US housing mortgages had been securitized — aggregated into mortgage-backed securities, whose income streams were \"tranched,\" providing higher-risk income streams to some investors and lower-risk income streams to others."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"This mechanism created severe moral hazard: loan originators no longer needed to care whether borrowers could repay, because loans would be immediately packaged and sold, with the risk borne by investors in the market. The result was a continuous decline in credit standards, and more and more borrowers who were originally unable to repay received loans."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Regulatory Vacuum","bold":true}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Investment banks and hedge funds, part of the \"Shadow Banking System,\" played an increasingly important role but were not subject to the same regulatory constraints as deposit-taking banks. These institutions used complex off-balance-sheet derivatives and securitization methods to conceal their true risk exposure from investors and regulators."}]},{"type":"hr","children":[{"type":"text","text":""}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Collapse: Interest Rate Hikes from 2004 Trigger Default Chain"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The Federal Reserve began raising interest rates in 2004, increasing the federal funds rate from 1% to 5.25–5.5% by June 2006."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"This directly hit a large number of subprime borrowers holding Adjustable Rate Mortgages (ARM) — they took out loans at extremely low \"teaser rates\" during low-interest periods, and after interest rate hikes, their monthly payments surged, making them unable to repay."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Default chain: Large-scale defaults by subprime borrowers → MBS values collapse → Financial institutions holding MBS suffer balance sheet damage → Institutions distrust each other (no one knows how many bad assets others hold) → Interbank market freezes → Credit system paralyzes."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"On September 15, 2008, Lehman Brothers filed for bankruptcy, becoming the landmark event of the full-blown crisis. This was an extreme case of the \"third way\" credit disappears as described in 1.3 — not due to massive defaults, but due to a systemic collapse of confidence, with credit across the entire financial system evaporating within weeks."}]},{"type":"hr","children":[{"type":"text","text":""}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Recovery: Why Faster Than Japan"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The US deleveraging process avoided Japan's mistakes at several critical junctures."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"① Rapidly acknowledge bad debts, force banks to clear them"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The Federal Reserve and Treasury Department forced major banks to disclose capital shortfalls through \"Stress Tests,\" injecting capital where needed and restructuring where necessary. This avoided the Japanese-style \"zombie banks\" dragging down the credit system for a long time."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"② Rapid, large-scale QE by the Federal Reserve"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The Federal Reserve cut interest rates to near zero by the end of 2008 and immediately launched large-scale quantitative easing: the first round of QE purchased approximately $1.75 trillion in assets, with subsequent two rounds totaling trillions, far exceeding the speed and scale of the Bank of Japan's actions back then."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"③ Sustained and sufficient fiscal stimulus"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"In 2009, the Obama administration launched the American Recovery and Reinvestment Act (ARRA) of approximately $787 billion, directly injecting demand into the economy, with coordinated fiscal and monetary policies."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"④ Learned from Japan's 1997 tax hike lesson, did not tighten too early"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The US did not rush to cut its fiscal deficit in the early stages of recovery, allowing deficits to remain high for a longer period until the economy found its footing."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Result Comparison","bold":true}]},{"type":"table","children":[{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Indicator"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"US Post-2008"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Japan Post-1989"}]}]}]},{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Stock market recovery"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Approx. 5 years (new high in 2013)"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Over 30 years, still not fully recovered"}]}]}]},{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"GDP recovery to pre-crisis level"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Approx. 3 years"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Over 10 years"}]}]}]},{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Inflation"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Remained positive, did not fall into deflation"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Long-term deflation"}]}]}]},{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Banking restructured"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Zombie banks dragged on for over a decade"}]}]}]},{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Deleveraging
