forces"}]}]}]}],"props":{"align":[]}},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Core Lessons","bold":true}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The essential difference between the two crises was not the size of the bubble, but the policy mix for deleveraging. Japan's deflationary forces (austerity + uncleared bad debts) long outweighed inflationary forces (monetary stimulus), and the mathematical condition for beautiful deleveraging (nominal income growth > debt interest rate) was never met; the US after 2008, through large-scale QE and fiscal stimulus, achieved a closer balance in its mix, coupled with rapid clearing of the banking system, which shortened the recovery time."}]},{"type":"h3","children":[{"type":"text","text":"7.3 Post-COVID Status of Three Countries (2025–2026)"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The COVID-19 pandemic was an exogenous shock, different from a credit cycle naturally peaking, but the way governments responded directly determined subsequent macroeconomic trends. The following analysis is based on the latest data as of April 2026."}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"United States: After a Soft Landing, New Uncertainties"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Policies During the Pandemic: Direct Payments to Residents","bold":true}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The key difference between the US pandemic response and 2008 was the flow of money. QE in 2008 involved money entering the financial system, then expected to transmit to the real economy through banks. During the pandemic, the US government directly issued cash checks (stimulus checks) to residents, coupled with large-scale unemployment benefits, bypassing the banking transmission link and directly driving a rebound in demand."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"This led to the inflation surge in 2021–2022: demand-side stimulus combined with supply chain disruptions (numerator increased, denominator decreased), with US CPI peaking at approximately 9% in mid-2022, the highest in forty years."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The Federal Reserve's Aggressive Interest Rate Hikes","bold":true}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"To curb inflation, the Federal Reserve raised interest rates at the fastest pace since the 1980s, from near 0% to 5.25–5.5% by 2023. The market constantly worried that rapid rate hikes would push the economy into recession, but the actual outcome was a soft landing — inflation receded, and the job market remained strong."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"In 2025, US GDP growth reached 2%, and inflation plateaued — tariffs pushed up commodity prices, while services inflation decreased, with the two offsetting each other. The fiscal deficit slightly narrowed from 6.3% of GDP to 5.9%, but total government debt rose to 123.9% of GDP."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"New Variable in 2025: Stagflationary Pressure from Tariffs","bold":true}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The Trump administration's large-scale tariff policy was the biggest exogenous variable in 2025. Tariffs are a typical cost-push inflation: rising import costs → shrinking denominator → rising prices. This is unrelated to domestic credit expansion, and interest rate hikes are not the right remedy, thus putting the Federal Reserve in a dilemma."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The CBO predicts real GDP growth of approximately 1.4% in 2025, lower than previous expectations, primarily dragged by tariff shocks; growth is expected to rebound to 2.2% in 2026, partly due to the short-term stimulus effect of tax cuts."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The IMF warns that current interest rates are already close to neutral, with almost no room for rate cuts in 2026, especially considering the upside risk of energy prices."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Judging from Dalio's Framework Position","bold":true}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The US is in a dual position of the late stage of the short-term debt cycle superimposed with the accumulation of long-term debt cycle pressure. The soft landing is real, but the debt-to-GDP ratio continues to rise, and fiscal deficits remain high, thinning the long-term debt cycle's buffer."}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Japan: Historic Turning Point, Yet Foundation Remains Unstable"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Bank of Japan Finally Begins Raising Interest Rates","bold":true}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The Bank of Japan raised its benchmark interest rate to 0.75% in December 2025, a 30-year high. Inflation has exceeded the 2% target for 44 consecutive months. This is the first time in three decades that the BOJ has implemented continuous interest rate hikes under real inflationary pressure, potentially marking a historic turning point from a long era of deflation."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"But Real Wages Remain Negative","bold":true}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Nominal contractual income growth accelerated to 2.2% in November 2025, but inflation-adjusted real wages still decreased by 2.8% year-on-year in November 2025. Nominal wages are rising, but inflation is running faster, and consumers' real purchasing power is still being eroded."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"This reveals a key issue: a significant proportion of Japan's current inflation comes from temporary cost-push factors like rising food prices, rather than the demand-driven inflation the central bank hopes to see. Among the two core inflation indicators, only the trimmed mean inflation barely exceeds the 2% target, and the BOJ itself admits that core inflation is still slowly approaching the 2% target."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The BOJ's Dilemma","bold":true}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The Bank of Japan maintained interest rates at 0.75% at its March 2026 meeting, with rhetoric indicating that uncertainties from the Middle East situation influenced the decision, and warned that rising oil prices could temporarily push up inflation while suppressing growth."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Wanting to raise rates but not daring to rush — this accurately describes the BOJ's current predicament: monetary policy normalization is the goal, but energy shocks, negative real wages, and uncertain external demand are all constraints."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"ING predicts the BOJ's next rate hike will be no earlier than October 2026; however, if the yen continues to depreciate and push up import prices, the timeline could be brought forward to the second quarter."}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"China: Deleveraging Underway, Systemic Deflationary Pressure Exists"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Surface Figures and Structural Divergence","bold":true}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"China's GDP growth in 2025 was approximately 5%, seemingly meeting the target, but the growth structure was severely imbalanced: exports and industrial production remained strong, while consumption and private investment remained sluggish, and fixed asset investment actually contracted."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Notably, one of the most important policy choices in 2025 was Beijing's decision not to pursue the old path of large-scale debt-driven stimulus, but instead to focus on manufacturing upgrades, green industries, and technological self-reliance."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Deflation is Already a Systemic Reality","bold":true}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The GDP deflator, which measures the overall price level, has been in continuous negative growth since 2023. China is facing a sustained deflation extremely rare among major economies since World War II, with the only comparable historical precedent being Japan's lost decades."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"In Dalio's framework, this is a dangerous signal. The deflation transmission chain:"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Asset prices fall (housing) → Household wealth shrinks (nominal terms) → Consumption willingness decreases → Corporate pricing power weakens → Prices fall → Deflationary expectations form → Consumption further postponed → Spiral strengthens"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Monetary Policy Transmission Breakdown","bold":true}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The People's Bank of China is easing monetary conditions, but the average actual lending rate has only decreased by about 4 basis points, with almost no substantial change. Against the backdrop of deflation and continuously falling producer prices, real interest rates are actually relatively high, further suppressing new investment willingness."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"This is highly similar to the core symptoms of Japan in the 1990s — monetary policy transmission broke down at the corporate borrowing willingness link."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Will China Follow Japan's Path?","bold":true}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The IMF explicitly points out that China's domestic demand remains weak, partly due to an unstable real estate market, which suppresses consumer confidence and creates deflationary pressure. The IMF recommends adopting more forceful expansionary policies and advancing social security system reforms to reduce residents' precautionary saving motives."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Similarities: Asset bubble burst (real estate), excessive debt, insufficient demand, deflationary pressure, aging population beginning."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Differences: The Chinese government's direct control over the economy is much stronger than Japan's, with more diverse policy tools; urbanization rate still has some room for improvement; however, the problem of \"getting old before getting rich\" is more severe than Japan's back then, and the window for policy adjustment is narrower."}]},{"type":"h3","children":[{"type":"text","text":"7.4 Middle East Conflict (2026): Exogenous Shock Superimposed on Fragile Foundations"}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Event Background"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"On February 28, 2026, Israel and the United States launched military strikes against Iran, after which Iran blockaded the Strait of Hormuz, cutting off approximately 20% of global oil trade routes. The International Energy Agency characterized this as the largest global energy supply disruption in history."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"On April 8, the US and Iran reached a two-week ceasefire agreement, brokered by Pakistan. The ceasefire framework required an immediate cessation of hostilities, reopening of the Strait of Hormuz, and entering a 15 to 20-day negotiation period. Both sides have violated the ceasefire since its inception."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"As of April 20, the ceasefire is set to expire on April 21, and a new round of negotiations is still underway, with the outcome undetermined."}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Positioning within the Framework"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"This is an extreme example of an \"exogenous supply-side shock.\""}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The Hormuz blockade directly compresses the denominator in the price equation:"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Sharp decrease in energy supply → Oil and LNG prices surge → Global production costs rise across the board (transportation, chemicals, electricity, agriculture) → Prices rise but without increased demand → Real purchasing power declines → Growth slows"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Result: Stagflation risk rises across the board, central bank tools have limited effectiveness."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The IMF's March 2026 assessment indicated that regardless of how the conflict evolves, all paths point to higher prices and slower growth. Energy is the main transmission channel, and for energy-importing countries, the effect is equivalent to imposing a huge hidden tax on all residents."}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Asymmetry of Impact"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The three major economies experienced vastly different degrees of impact:"}]},{"type":"table","children":[{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"United States"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Japan"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"China"}]}]}]},{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Direct The two are not contradictory; they are different facets of the same reality."}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Cross-Section vs. Trend"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Daily perception sees a "},{"type":"text","text":"cross-section","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":": the consumption behavior and mental state of these people, at this moment, in this place."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Macroeconomic forecasting looks at "},{"type":"text","text":"trends","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":": the direction of change in wage growth, debt burden, demographic structure, and asset prices over a scale of years to decades."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Macroeconomic deterioration is often not as suddenly visible as a crisis; it's more like a frog in slowly boiling water — each day is manageable, but looking back ten years later, living standards have quietly declined. For most of Japan's lost three decades, Tokyo's cafes were full, and young people were out shopping."}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Structural Reasons for Japanese Youth's Active Consumption"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Japanese youth pursue fashion and consume actively, backed by real structural logic:"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Not buying homes, not buying cars, not getting married, not having children (these large expenditures are systematically contracting) → Money saved is instead used for immediate enjoyment: dining, travel, fashion, concerts"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"This is rational intertemporal substitution — since the future doesn't look better than the present, and long-term asset (real estate) return expectations are low, it's better to concentrate spending on consumption that can be enjoyed now. This is not a sign of \"being rich,\" but a rational adaptation to long-term pessimistic expectations."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"From the perspective of nominal vs. real values: young people's nominal consumption may be increasing, but real wages are negative, and real savings rates are declining — consumption appears active on paper, but purchasing power is actually being eroded."}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Selection Bias"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"In Japan's Tokyo metropolitan area, well-educated young people with stable jobs belong to a relatively secure class. Their situation does not represent:"}]},{"type":"ul","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The actual situation of local economies (continuous population outflow, business contraction)"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The condition of small manufacturing towns"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The living pressure of the informally employed population (approximately 40% of Japan's labor force)"}]}]}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Macro data captures the whole, while daily observations are limited by the homogeneity of social circles. The superposition of the two easily leads to systematic over-optimism bias."}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"A Useful Habit for Verification"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Whenever your intuition tells you \"things feel pretty good,\" you can check against the following questions:"}]},{"type":"ul","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Are real wages (nominal wage growth - inflation rate) rising or falling?"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Where does the debt burden (debt repayment amount / income) stand historically?"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Is the consumption you see supported by income, or by reduced savings or borrowing?"}]}]}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The answers to these three questions are often closer to macroeconomic reality than sensory impressions."}]}]},"reading":null},{"_sys":{"filename":"basic-concepts-of-economics","basename":"basic-concepts-of-economics.mdx","hasReferences":null,"breadcrumbs":["en","macroeconomics","basic-concepts-of-economics"],"path":"content/notes/en/macroeconomics/basic-concepts-of-economics.mdx","relativePath":"en/macroeconomics/basic-concepts-of-economics.mdx","extension":".mdx"},"id":"content/notes/en/macroeconomics/basic-concepts-of-economics.mdx","__typename":"NoteNote","createdAt":"2026-04-22T11:20:02.178Z","updatedAt":"2026-04-26T07:21:46.700Z","author":{"__typename":"Author","createdAt":"2026-01-08T16:27:13.341Z","updatedAt":"2026-01-17T13:20:25.697Z","name":"Toshinaki","pronouns":"♂","avatar":"/uploads/authors/avatars/ChatGPT-Image-Jun-23,-2026,-06_02_44-PM.png","avatarAlt":"Toshinaki","tagline":["Creator","Lifelong Learner","Aspiring Digital Nomad"],"jobTitle":"Programmer (frontend-first, backend in progress)","company":"Stockmark Inc.","intro":"An older single guy. Birth, old age, sickness, death — that is the half decreed by heaven, beyond anyone's will, that no one escapes. The other half — not getting what you want, parting from those you love, meeting those you hate, and that “blaze of the five aggregates” — is all born of the human heart. Being emotional, I am too easily moved, and so too easily wounded. The world is impermanent; in life, eight or nine times out of ten things do not go your way. And so suffering follows me like a shadow."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Over time I have worked out a few ways to cope with it: distracting myself, which is what I have consciously tried; and “reverse philosophical suicide,” which is what I have unconsciously been doing all along."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"At this age, looking back, I don't think reason was something I was born with. It feels more like a choice — I chose to keep myself rational, to push back against feeling, against the pain that feeling brings. To be honest, it has not worked very well."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Camus held that “the absurd” arises from the collision between “man's longing for meaning” and “the silence of the world.” Faced with a universe that offers no meaning at all, you either commit physical suicide and end the suffering at its root — a coward's compromise; or you commit “philosophical suicide,” throwing yourself into faith (the leap of faith) to grant meaning by force — a betrayal of reason."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"I tried to distract myself. What is Economics"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The core question economics studies: "},{"type":"text","text":"how individuals and societies make choices under conditions of limited resources.","bold":true}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The keyword in this definition is "},{"type":"text","text":"scarcity","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":": human wants are unlimited, but the resources needed to satisfy them are limited. The opposite is a free resource—one that is, in some sense, almost infinitely abundant, and one person's use does not affect others'."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The object of study in economics is scarce resources, including: food, water, labor, land, time, capital."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The boundary of free resources is relative—atmospheric oxygen on Earth is a free resource for ordinary people, but a scarce resource for residents of a lunar base. economic analysis, permeating all discussions."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Positive Statement","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":": A statement that can be tested, describing \"what is,\" independent of personal values. It may be true or false, but can be verified through data and experiments."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Example:","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" \"Welfare programs reduce people's work incentives\"—this can be tested by comparing data from groups with and without welfare programs, regardless of whether the conclusion is true or false."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Normative Statement","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":": A statement based on value judgments, describing \"what ought to be,\" an opinion or moral stance that cannot be objectively tested."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Example:","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" \"Giving welfare to people unwilling to work is wrong and unfair\"—this involves value judgments and cannot be tested for right or wrong with data."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"⚠️ Identification Tip: Statements containing \"should,\" \"fair/unfair,\" or \"right/wrong\" are usually normative statements; other statements, as long as they describe observable cause-and-effect relationships, are usually positive statements—even if the speaker has a clear value bias."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Example:","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" \"Taxing the rich will promote economic growth\" and \"Taxing the rich will inhibit economic growth\" are opposite in stance, but both are positive statements—because both are testable causal claims."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Disagreements among economists usually stem from: ① different empirical judgments about positive statements, or ② different normative stances. Distinguishing these two types of disagreements helps understand the true nature of the debate."}]},{"type":"h3","children":[{"type":"text","text":"1.4 Economic Models"}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"What is a Model"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Economics borrows from other sciences—using "},{"type":"text","text":"simplified models","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" to understand complex systems."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Chemistry has the ideal gas law, simplifying the interactions of trillions of particles into equations with a few variables; biology uses fruit flies and mice to study problems that cannot be directly experimented on humans; economics also uses models—establishing an analytical framework in complex reality through simplifying assumptions and mathematization."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Common simplifying assumptions in economic models include:"}]},{"type":"ul","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"All agents are rational"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Everyone has the same or complete information"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"All other things being equal (Ceteris Paribus, meaning \"other conditions being the same\")"}]}]}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Value of Models"}]},{"type":"ul","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Clarifies thinking, allowing for mathematical expression and derivation"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Can identify relationships between key variables"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Can make predictions for policy discussions"}]}]}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Limitations of Models"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"This is a more important understanding than the models themselves:"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"1. Models are based on assumptions, and assumptions can be wrong","bold":true}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"\"Everyone is rational\" does not hold true in reality. The entire field of Behavioral Economics is built on studying human \"irrational\" choices."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"2. Macroeconomic models are particularly dangerous","bold":true}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The micro level already simplifies complex human behavior once; the macro level aggregates the behavior of millions of people on top of that—uncertainty is amplified exponentially."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"3. Economics cannot conduct experiments like physics","bold":true}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"You cannot randomly divide a thousand similar economies into two groups, apply a policy to one, and then observe and compare the results. This makes determining causality inherently more difficult than in physics or chemistry."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"⚠️ "},{"type":"text","text":"Mathematics may appear rigorous, but it does not guarantee correct conclusions.","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" If the assumptions themselves are flawed, even precise mathematics merely builds a precise edifice of error on a faulty foundation. Always ask: What are the core assumptions of this model? Are they valid in the current context?"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Two maxims worth remembering:"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"\"An economist is an expert who states the obvious in terms of the incomprehensible.\"—Alfred Knopf"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"\"An economist is an expert who will know tomorrow why the things he predicted yesterday didn't happen today.\"—Lawrence J. (units)"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Berries Economic growth refers to the entire curve shifting outward."}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Curve Shape and Types of Opportunity Cost"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The shape of the PPC reveals the pattern of opportunity cost:"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"img","url":"/uploads/notes/oc.png","caption":null,"children":[{"type":"text","text":""}]}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"1. Constant Opportunity Cost","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":"\nPPC is a straight line. For each additional unit of one good produced, the quantity of the other good sacrificed remains constant.\n"},{"type":"text","text":"Example:","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" For each rabbit caught, 60 berries are always sacrificed."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"2. Increasing Opportunity Cost","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":"\nPPC is bowed outward (convex to the origin). As the production of one good increases, the quantity of the other good that must be sacrificed for each additional unit increases. "},{"type":"text","text":"This is the most common shape.","bold":true}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"3. Decreasing Opportunity Cost","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":"\nPPC is bowed inward (concave to the origin). As the production of one good increases, the quantity of the other good that must be sacrificed for each additional unit decreases.\nReason: Economies of scale, learning effects—becoming more proficient, marginal cost decreases."}]},{"type":"h2","children":[{"type":"text","text":"2.2 Opportunity Cost"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Opportunity Cost: The value of the "},{"type":"text","text":"best alternative","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" sacrificed when making a choice."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Cost in economics is not just monetary cost, but \"what you had to give up\"."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Example:","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":"\nIn scenario E (1 rabbit, 280 berries), wanting to catch 1 more rabbit (moving to scenario D):"}]},{"type":"ul","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Rabbits +1 (from 1 to 2)"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Berries -40 (from 280 to 240)"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Therefore: Opportunity cost of catching 1 more rabbit = 40 berries"}]}]}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Marginal Cost"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Marginal Cost: The opportunity cost of producing "},{"type":"text","text":"1 additional unit","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" of a good."}]},{"type":"code_block","value":"机会成本（每单位X）= ΔY ÷ ΔX\n                  = (Y₁ - Y₂) ÷ (X₂ - X₁)","children":[{"type":"code_line","children":[{"text":"机会成本（每单位X）= ΔY ÷ ΔX"}]},{"type":"code_line","children":[{"text":"                  = (Y₁ - Y₂) ÷ (X₂ - X₁)"}]}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Example:","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":"\nFrom scenario E (1 rabbit, 280 berries) to scenario D (2 rabbits, 240 "},{"type":"text","text":"moves from a point on the curve to a point inside the curve","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":", representing underutilization of existing resources. The curve itself does not move; rather, the economy's operating point deviates from the efficiency frontier."}]},{"type":"h3","children":[{"type":"text","text":"2.4 Connection to Macroeconomic Frameworks"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The PPC is the most intuitive model for understanding "},{"type":"text","text":"scarcity","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" and "},{"type":"text","text":"trade-offs","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":", transforming the abstract concept of \"limited resources\" into a quantifiable and visualizable tool."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"In subsequent analyses, the PPC way of thinking will appear in different forms:"}]},{"type":"ul","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The \"guns vs. butter\" trade-off (military spending vs. civilian spending) in fiscal policy discussions is a typical PPC trade-off."}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"A country's long-term economic growth path is essentially a process of the PPC continuously shifting outward—this perfectly corresponds to Dalio's framework where \"productivity growth is the sole source of long-term wealth\"."}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Recession (operating inside the PPC) corresponds to the credit contraction and idle resource phases in Dalio's framework."}]}]}]},{"type":"h2","children":[{"type":"text","text":"3. Comparative Advantage and Gains from Trade"}]},{"type":"h3","children":[{"type":"text","text":"3.1 Comparative Advantage is the True Basis for Specialization"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"img","url":"/uploads/notes/ca-trade.png","caption":null,"children":[{"type":"text","text":""}]}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Suppose Country A and Country B can both produce pants and shirts. Conversely, Country B only needs to give up 2/3 pairs of pants to produce 1 shirt, while Country A needs to give up 2 pairs—"},{"type":"text","text":"Country B has a comparative advantage in shirts","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":"."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"This conclusion is graphically represented by the slope of the PPC: Country A's slope (absolute value) is smaller, indicating fewer shirts are sacrificed for each additional pair of pants produced. The difference in the slopes of the two lines is the intuitive source of comparative advantage."}]},{"type":"h3","children":[{"type":"text","text":"3.2 Specialization, Terms of Trade, and Gains from Trade"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Once comparative advantage is clear, the specialization plan is determined: Country A specializes in producing pants, and Country B specializes in producing shirts."}]},{"type":"ul","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Country A fully produces pants: 20 pairs/day, 0 shirts"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Country B fully produces shirts: 0 pairs of pants, 45 units/day"}]}]}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Both countries need each other's goods, leading to trade. For Country A, producing 1 shirt itself means giving up 2 pairs of pants; as long as the trade price is less than 2 pairs of pants, buying shirts from Country B is more cost-effective than producing them itself. For Country B, producing 1 pair of pants itself means giving up 3/2 shirts; as long as the trade price is less than 3/2 shirts, buying pants from Country A is more cost-effective than producing them itself."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Therefore, the reasonable terms of trade range is:"}]},{"type":"code_block","value":"A国机会成本 < 裤子价格 < B国机会成本\n1/2件衬衫 < 裤子价格 < 3/2件衬衫","children":[{"type":"code_line","children":[{"text":"A国机会成本 < 裤子价格 < B国机会成本"}]},{"type":"code_line","children":[{"text":"1/2件衬衫 < 裤子价格 < 3/2件衬衫"}]}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Taking a midpoint, both parties agree to trade at "},{"type":"text","text":"1 pair of pants for 1 shirt","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":"."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Suppose Country A trades 15 pairs of pants for 15 shirts. This again illustrates that absolute advantage and comparative advantage are two different things, and the basis for specialization is always opportunity cost, not the level of output."}]},{"type":"h3","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Connection to Macroeconomic Frameworks"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The theory of comparative advantage explains why international specialization and trade can increase global total output—this directly corresponds to Dalio's framework where \"productivity growth is the sole source of long-term wealth\": specialization itself is a form of productivity enhancement. When the trade system is impacted (tariff wars, shipping blockades), countries are forced to abandon comparative advantage and turn to inefficient self-sufficiency, leading to a decline in global productivity. This is the underlying mechanism by which trade friction drags down economic growth."}]},{"type":"h2","children":[{"type":"text","text":"4. Demand"}]},{"type":"h3","children":[{"type":"text","text":"4.1 Demand and Quantity Demanded"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"When studying demand theory, the easiest mistake to make is confusing two concepts:"}]},{"type":"ul","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Demand","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" is the entire curve, describing all quantities buyers are willing and able to purchase at all possible prices—that is, the complete relationship between price and quantity demanded."}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Quantity Demanded","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" is a specific point on the curve, corresponding to the particular quantity buyers are willing to purchase at a given price."}]}]}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Their mechanisms of change are completely different:"}]},{"type":"ul","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Change in Quantity Demanded","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":": Caused by a change in price, represented by a movement along the same curve; the demand curve itself does not shift."}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Change in Demand","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":": Caused by factors other than price, resulting in a shift of the entire demand curve—a shift to the right indicates an increase in demand (higher quantity demanded at any given price), and a shift to the left indicates a decrease in demand."}]}]}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"💡 Rule of Thumb: If a change alters the quantity buyers are willing to purchase at "},{"type":"text","text":"any given price","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":", it is a change in demand (curve shift); if it only changes the price itself, it is a change in quantity demanded (movement along the curve)."}]},{"type":"h3","children":[{"type":"text","text":"4.2 Demand Curve"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"A "},{"type":"text","text":"Demand Schedule","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" is a table listing the quantity demanded at various prices, serving as the data source for the demand curve."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The "},{"type":"text","text":"Demand Curve","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" plots the demand schedule, with price on the vertical axis and quantity on the horizontal axis, appearing as a downward-sloping curve from top-left to bottom-right."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Law of Demand","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":": Under the assumption of Ceteris Paribus (all other things being equal), there is an inverse relationship between price and quantity demanded—as price rises, quantity demanded falls; as price falls, quantity demanded rises."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"img","url":"/uploads/notes/qd.png","caption":null,"children":[{"type":"text","text":""}]}]},{"type":"h3","children":[{"type":"text","text":"4.3 Determinants of Demand"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The following five categories of factors (mnemonic "},{"type":"text","text":"TONIE","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":") can shift the entire demand curve. Note that this is a primary framework, not an exhaustive list—any factor that changes the quantity buyers are willing to purchase at a given price will cause the curve to shift."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"img","url":"/uploads/notes/demand-shift.png","caption":null,"children":[{"type":"text","text":""}]}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"T — Tastes"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"When consumer preferences for a good change, demand changes accordingly. Increased preference (e.g., advertising effect, social trend) → demand shifts right; decreased preference → demand shifts left."}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"O — Price of Other Goods"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Related goods fall into two categories, with opposite effects:"}]},{"type":"ul","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Substitute Goods","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":": Goods that can be used in place of each other; consumers choose between them. Price of a substitute rises → the good in question becomes relatively more attractive → demand for the good shifts right; price of a substitute falls → demand shifts left."}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Complement Goods","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":": Goods typically consumed together, indispensable to each other. Price of a complement rises → total cost of the combination rises → demand for the good shifts left; price of a complement falls → demand shifts right."}]}]}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Example:","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" Ebooks and e-readers (Kindle) are complements. If the price of Kindle rises, fewer people buy Kindles, which in turn causes the demand for ebooks to fall, shifting the demand curve left."}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"N — Number of Buyers"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"An increase in potential buyers in the market (e.g., population growth, opening of new markets) → quantity demanded at any price increases → demand shifts right; decrease in buyers → demand shifts left."}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"I — Income"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The effect of income on demand depends on the type of good:"}]},{"type":"ul","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Normal Good","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":": Income increases → demand increases (shifts right); income decreases → demand decreases (shifts left). Most goods fall into this category; laptops, restaurant dining, etc., are typical examples."}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Inferior Good","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":": Income increases → demand decreases (shifts left); income decreases → demand increases (shifts right). \"Inferior\" does not mean poor quality, but rather that when income rises, consumers switch to better substitutes, causing demand for the good to fall. A typical example is the cheapest car on the market—when income increases, people choose better models instead of buying more cheap cars."}]}]}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"img","url":"/uploads/notes/inferior-good.png","caption":null,"children":[{"type":"text","text":""}]}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"E — Expectations"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"When consumers' expectations about future prices change:"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Expectations of future price increases → tendency to buy sooner → current demand shifts right; expectations of future price decreases → tendency to postpone purchases → current demand shifts left."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Example:","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" Consumers of electronics (phones, computers) generally expect prices to continue falling, which to some extent suppresses current demand—people wait for lower prices before buying."}]},{"type":"h3","children":[{"type":"text","text":"4.4 Common Misconceptions"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Misconception 1: Price changes cause changes in demand.","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":"\nPrice changes only cause changes in quantity demanded (movement along the curve), not changes in demand itself (no curve shift). Only TONIE factors shift the curve."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Misconception 2: A change in a good's price affects its own demand curve.","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":"\nA change in the price of a good changes its quantity demanded, and the demand curves of related goods (substitutes, complements), but not its own demand curve."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Example:","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" When the price of hot dogs falls, three things happen simultaneously: the quantity demanded of hot dogs increases (movement along its own curve); the demand curve for hot dog buns (complement) shifts right; the demand curve for hamburgers (substitute) shifts left."}]},{"type":"h3","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Connection to Macroeconomic Frameworks"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The equation \"Total Spending = Money + Credit\" in Dalio's framework can be understood in the context of demand theory as follows: credit expansion is equivalent to an increase in effective income (I in TONIE), pushing the demand curves for normal goods to the right, driving up prices, and creating inflationary pressure during expansionary periods; credit contraction, conversely, shifts demand curves left, reducing price pressure or even leading to deflation. The macroeconomic credit cycle is manifested at the micro level through the simultaneous shifts of countless demand curves in the same direction."}]},{"type":"h2","children":[{"type":"text","text":"5. Supply"}]},{"type":"h3","children":[{"type":"text","text":"5.1 Supply and Quantity Supplied"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Similar to the demand section, the core distinction in supply theory is:"}]},{"type":"ul","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Supply","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" is the entire curve, describing all quantities sellers are willing and able to produce at all possible prices—that is, the complete relationship between price and quantity supplied."}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Quantity Supplied","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" is a specific point on the curve, corresponding to the particular quantity sellers are willing to produce at a given price."}]}]}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Their mechanisms of change:"}]},{"type":"ul","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Change in Quantity Supplied","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":": Caused by a change in price, represented by a movement along the same curve; the supply curve itself does not shift."}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Change in Supply","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":": Caused by factors other than price, resulting in a shift of the entire supply curve—a shift to the right indicates an increase in supply (higher quantity supplied at any given price), and a shift to the left indicates a decrease in supply."}]}]}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"💡 Rule of Thumb is identical to demand: If a change alters the quantity sellers are willing to produce at any given price, it is a change in supply (curve shift); if it only changes the price itself, it is a change in quantity supplied (movement along the curve)."}]},{"type":"h3","children":[{"type":"text","text":"5.2 Supply Curve"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"A "},{"type":"text","text":"Supply Schedule","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" is a table listing the quantity supplied at various prices, serving as the data source for the supply curve."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The "},{"type":"text","text":"Supply Curve","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" plots the supply schedule, with price on the vertical axis and quantity on the horizontal axis, and unlike the demand curve, appears as an "},{"type":"text","text":"upward-sloping","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" curve from bottom-left to top-right."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Law of Supply","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":": Under the assumption of Ceteris Paribus (all other things being equal), there is a positive relationship between price and quantity supplied—as price rises, quantity supplied rises; as price falls, quantity supplied falls."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The core difference between the Law of Supply and the Law of Demand: the demand curve slopes downward (negative relationship), while the supply curve slopes upward (positive relationship). This is intuitively understandable: the higher the price, the greater the producer's profit, and the stronger the incentive to produce."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"img","url":"/uploads/notes/qs.png","caption":null,"children":[{"type":"text","text":""}]}]},{"type":"h3","children":[{"type":"text","text":"5.3 Determinants of Supply"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"img","url":"/uploads/notes/supply-shift.png","caption":null,"children":[{"type":"text","text":""}]}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The following six categories of factors can shift the entire supply curve:"}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Price of Inputs"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Changes in the prices of raw materials, labor, land, energy, and other inputs required to produce a good directly affect production costs."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Input prices rise → production costs rise → profits decrease at any given price → willingness to produce decreases → supply shifts left;\nInput prices fall → production costs fall → supply shifts right."}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Price of Related Goods"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"⚠️ Note: \"Related goods\" on the supply side have a different meaning than on the demand side. Here, it refers to "},{"type":"text","text":"production substitutes","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":"—other goods that producers can make with the same resources."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"If the price of a related good rises → producers shift resources to that good → supply of the good in question shifts left;\nIf the price of a related good falls → supply of the good in question shifts right."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Example:","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" A grape farmer can choose to grow grapes or blueberries on their land. If blueberry prices rise, the farmer will shift more land to blueberries, and grape supply will consequently decrease."}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Number of Sellers"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"An increase in the number of producers in the market → total supply increases (shifts right); decrease in producers → total supply decreases (shifts left). This is a direct aggregate effect at the market level."}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Technology"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Technological advancements allow for more output with the same inputs, or reduce production costs."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Technological improvement → supply shifts right; technological regression (rare) → supply shifts left."}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Government Policy (Government Regulation, Subsidies, Taxes)"}]},{"type":"ul","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Government subsidy → lowers production costs → supply shifts right"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Government tax → raises production costs → supply shifts left"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Deregulation → supply shifts right; stricter regulation → supply shifts left"}]}]}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Expectations of Future Prices"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Similar to the demand side, but with different directional logic:"}]},{"type":"ul","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"If producers expect future prices to rise → tend to hoard current stock, reduce current sales → current supply shifts left"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"If producers expect future prices to fall → tend to accelerate sales → current supply shifts right"}]}]}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"⚠️ This logic only applies to "},{"type":"text","text":"storable goods","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" (e.g., oil, metals). For perishable goods (e.g., grapes), storage is not possible, so price expectations have limited impact on current supply."}]},{"type":"h3","children":[{"type":"text","text":"5.4 Comparison of Determinants of Supply and Demand"}]},{"type":"table","children":[{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Demand"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Supply"}]}]}]},{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Curve supply"}]}]}]},{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Price expectations"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Expected price increase → current demand shifts right"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Expected price increase → current supply shifts left"}]}]}]},{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Number of right"}]}]}]}],"props":{"align":[]}},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The row on price expectations is particularly noteworthy: demand and supply react in "},{"type":"text","text":"opposite","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" directions to the same expectation (future price increase)—consumers buy sooner (demand increases), while producers hoard and withhold sales (supply decreases)."}]},{"type":"h3","children":[{"type":"text","text":"5.5 Common Misconceptions"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Misconception: Price increases cause supply to increase.","bold":true}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"A price increase only leads to an increase in quantity supplied (movement upward along the curve); the supply curve itself does not change. Only changes in non-price factors cause the entire supply curve to shift (supply increase or decrease)."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"This is perfectly symmetrical to the misconception in the demand section—price changes only cause changes in \"quantity,\" not changes in the \"curve.\""}]},{"type":"h3","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Connection to Macroeconomic Frameworks"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The supply curve at the macroeconomic level corresponds to the economy's productive capacity. Technological advancement and productivity growth mentioned in Dalio's framework are manifested at the micro level as a continuous rightward shift of the supply curve—more output can be provided at the same price. Imported inflation (e.g., rising oil prices) is a macroeconomic manifestation of rising input prices leading to a leftward shift of the supply curve: the essence of cost-push inflation is that the supply curves of the entire economy simultaneously shift left, pushing up the price level while demand remains unchanged."}]},{"type":"h2","children":[{"type":"text","text":"6. Market and Equilibrium"}]},{"type":"h3","children":[{"type":"text","text":"6.1 Market Equilibrium"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"A "},{"type":"text","text":"Market","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" is an interactive mechanism where buyers and sellers exchange goods, services, or resources. When the demand curve and supply curve are plotted on the same graph, their intersection point is the market's "},{"type":"text","text":"Equilibrium","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":"."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Equilibrium Price","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":": The price at which quantity demanded equals quantity supplied, also known as the \"Market Clearing Price\"."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Equilibrium Quantity","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":": The actual quantity traded at the equilibrium price."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Meaning of the equilibrium point: At this price, all goods that sellers are willing to sell at this price find buyers, and all buyers willing to purchase at this price find goods. The market clears perfectly, with no surplus and no shortage."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"img","url":"/uploads/notes/market-equilibrium.png","caption":null,"children":[{"type":"text","text":""}]}]},{"type":"h3","children":[{"type":"text","text":"6.2 Disequilibrium: Shortage and Surplus"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"When the market price deviates from the equilibrium price, the market is in a state of "},{"type":"text","text":"Disequilibrium","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":", manifested as either a shortage or a surplus. Market forces will spontaneously push the price back towards equilibrium."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Shortage","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":": When the price is below the equilibrium price, quantity demanded > quantity supplied."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Price too low → many buyers, few sellers → goods are snapped up, supply cannot meet demand → buyers bid up prices → price rises → quantity supplied increases, quantity demanded decreases → moves towards equilibrium"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Surplus","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":": When the price is above the equilibrium price, quantity supplied > quantity demanded."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Price too high → many sellers, few buyers → goods accumulate unsold → sellers are forced to lower prices → quantity demanded increases, quantity supplied decreases → moves towards equilibrium"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"💡 Core of market mechanism: Shortages push prices up, surpluses push prices down, and both forces point towards the equilibrium point. This is the basic logic of how the price mechanism spontaneously allocates resources."}]},{"type":"h3","children":[{"type":"text","text":"6.3 Changes in Equilibrium"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"When the determinants of supply or demand change, the curves shift, and the equilibrium point changes accordingly, forming a new equilibrium price and quantity."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Results of a single curve shift are definite:","bold":true}]},{"type":"table","children":[{"type":"tr","children":[{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Change"}]}]},{"type":"td","children":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Equilibrium supply and demand change simultaneously, results are partially uncertain:","bold":true}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"When both supply and demand change simultaneously, the "},{"type":"text","text":"same","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":", the direction of quantity is certain, and price is uncertain; when the direction of supply and demand changes is "},{"type":"text","text":"opposite","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":", the direction of price is certain, and quantity is to Macroeconomic Frameworks"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The market equilibrium mechanism is the microeconomic foundation of the price signaling function in Dalio's framework. Macroeconomic inflation is essentially the result of a large number of markets in the entire economy simultaneously experiencing increased demand (credit expansion pushing demand curves right) or decreased supply (cost shocks pushing supply curves left)—the price increases in each individual market, when aggregated, lead to an increase in the overall price level."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Central banks use interest rate hikes to suppress demand (shifting the demand curve left), precisely leveraging the market equilibrium mechanism to lower price levels. Gross Domestic Product (GDP)"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Gross Domestic Product (GDP)","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":" is the most commonly used indicator to measure the size of a country's economy."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Complete definition: "},{"type":"text","text":"The market value of all final goods and services produced within a country's geographical boundaries during a given period.","bold":true}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Each word in the definition has a specific meaning and is indispensable:"}]},{"type":"ul","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Market Value","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":": Output is measured by price, not quantity. The value of this model lies in revealing the logical structure, rather than precisely describing reality."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"In this model, money and real flows move in opposite directions, forming a closed loop:"}]},{"type":"ul","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Real flow (clockwise): Factors of production flow from households to firms; goods and services flow from firms back to households."}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Money flow (counter-clockwise): Consumption expenditure flows from households to firms; factor income (wages, rent, interest, profit) flows from firms back to households."}]}]}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"img","url":"/uploads/notes/circular-flow.png","caption":null,"children":[{"type":"text","text":""}]}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"In this closed loop","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":", because households are the sole providers of all factors of production and the sole purchasers of all goods and services:"}]},{"type":"ul","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Household expenditure = Firm revenue"}]}]},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"lic","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Firm revenue = Firm costs (wages + rent + interest) + profit = Household income"}]}]}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"⚠️ The second equality holds on the premise that \"profits also belong to households\"—that is, firms are owned by households, and profits ultimately flow back to households. This is a model assumption, not a universal fact."}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Therefore, in this model: "},{"type":"text","text":"Total Expenditure = Total Income = GDP","bold":true},{"type":"text","text":", and the following three methods of measuring GDP yield the same result."}]},{"type":"h3","children":[{"type":"text","text":"1.2 Three Methods of Measuring GDP"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The three methods observe the same economic activity from different perspectives, and the results are completely equivalent. The sum of the demand side ("},{"type":"text","text":"C+I+G+X-M","code":true},{"type":"text","text":") and the production side (durables/non-durables/services/construction/inventories) also yields exactly the same result—because every market transaction must have both a buyer and a seller, the total amount purchased must equal the total amount produced."}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"Method One: Expenditures Approach"}]},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","text":"The most commonly used method. Under the value-added method, China's GDP increases by $30, and the US GDP increases by $20 (only counting the value newly added within the US). If using the final product method, only the US $50 is included in US GDP, and China's $30 is not double-counted—the two methods yield consistent results, but the value-added method is clearer when tracking cross-border supply chains."}]},{"type":"h3","children":[{"type":"text","text":"1.3 Components of the Expenditures Approach"}]},{"type":"h4","children":[{"type":"text","text":"C:
